         <img alt="File:Iorotateing1day.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Iorotateing1day.ogv/220px--Iorotateing1day.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Iorotateing1day.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iorotateing1day.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Rotating image of Io's surface; the large red ring is around the volcano
         <a href="/wiki/Pele_(volcano)" title="Pele (volcano)">
          Pele
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Based on their experience with the ancient surfaces of the Moon, Mars, and Mercury, scientists expected to see numerous
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact craters
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        Voyager 1
       </i>
       's first images of Io. The density of impact craters across Io's surface would have given clues to Io's age. However, they were surprised to discover that the surface was almost completely lacking in impact craters, but was instead covered in smooth plains dotted with tall mountains, pits of various shapes and sizes, and volcanic lava flows.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1979_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1979-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Compared to most worlds observed to that point, Io's surface was covered in a variety of colorful materials (leading Io to be compared to a rotten
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">
        orange
       </a>
       or to
       <a href="/wiki/Pizza" title="Pizza">
        pizza
       </a>
       ) from various sulfurous compounds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt2000_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Britt2000-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calder2005_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Calder2005-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The lack of impact craters indicated that Io's surface is geologically young, like the terrestrial surface; volcanic materials continuously bury craters as they are produced. This result was spectacularly confirmed as at least nine active volcanoes were observed by
       <i>
        Voyager 1
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom1979_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Strom1979-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_composition">
        Surface composition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Io_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Surface composition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Io's colorful appearance is the result of materials deposited by its extensive volcanism, including
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicates
       </a>
       (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">
        orthopyroxene
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
        sulfur
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">
        sulfur dioxide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap9_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap9-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sulfur dioxide frost is ubiquitous across the surface of Io, forming large regions covered in white or grey materials. Sulfur is also seen in many places across Io, forming yellow to yellow-green regions. Sulfur deposited in the mid-latitude and polar regions is often damaged by radiation, breaking up the normally stable
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclooctasulfur" title="Cyclooctasulfur">
        cyclic 8-chain sulfur
       </a>
       . This radiation damage produces Io's red-brown polar regions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnard1894_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Barnard1894-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1169" data-file-width="2281" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg/220px-First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg/330px-First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg/440px-First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_Geologic_Map_of_Jupiter%E2%80%99s_Moon_Io.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Geological map of Io
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanism_on_Io#Plumes" title="Volcanism on Io">
        Explosive volcanism
       </a>
       , often taking the form of umbrella-shaped plumes, paints the surface with sulfurous and silicate materials. Plume deposits on Io are often colored red or white depending on the amount of sulfur and sulfur dioxide in the plume. Generally, plumes formed at volcanic vents from degassing lava contain a greater amount of S
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       , producing a red "fan" deposit, or in extreme cases, large (often reaching beyond 450 km or 280 mi from the central vent) red rings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2000b_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2000b-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A prominent example of a red-ring plume deposit is located at Pele. These red deposits consist primarily of sulfur (generally 3- and 4-chain molecular sulfur), sulfur dioxide, and perhaps
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfuryl_chloride" title="Sulfuryl chloride">
        sulfuryl chloride
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap9_81-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap9-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Plumes formed at the margins of silicate lava flows (through the interaction of lava and pre-existing deposits of sulfur and sulfur dioxide) produce white or gray deposits.
      </p>
      <p>
       Compositional mapping and Io's high density suggest that Io contains little to no
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       , though small pockets of water ice or
       <a href="/wiki/Mineral_hydration" title="Mineral hydration">
        hydrated minerals
       </a>
       have been tentatively identified, most notably on the northwest flank of the mountain
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gish_Bar_Mons&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gish Bar Mons (page does not exist)">
        Gish Bar Mons
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doute2004_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Doute2004-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Io has the least amount of water of any known body in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seeds2012_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Seeds2012-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This lack of water is likely due to Jupiter being hot enough early in the
       <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">
        evolution of the Solar System
       </a>
       to drive off
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        volatile materials
       </a>
       like water in the vicinity of Io, but not hot enough to do so farther out.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrobio20140306_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-astrobio20140306-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">
        Volcanism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Io_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Volcanism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Volcanology_of_Io" title="Volcanology of Io">
        Volcanology of Io
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_volcanic_features_on_Io" title="List of volcanic features on Io">
        List of volcanic features on Io
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tvastarpic2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="682" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Tvastarpic2.jpg/300px-Tvastarpic2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Tvastarpic2.jpg/450px-Tvastarpic2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Tvastarpic2.jpg/600px-Tvastarpic2.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tvastarpic2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Active lava flows in volcanic region
         <a href="/wiki/Tvashtar_Paterae" title="Tvashtar Paterae">
          Tvashtar Paterae
         </a>
         (blank region represents saturated areas in the original data). Images taken by
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         in November 1999 and February 2000.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The tidal heating produced by Io's forced
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricity
       </a>
       has made it the most volcanically active world in the Solar System, with hundreds of volcanic centres and extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">
        lava flows
       </a>
       . During a major eruption, lava flows tens or even hundreds of kilometres long can be produced, consisting mostly of
       <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">
        basalt
       </a>
       silicate lavas with either
       <a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">
        mafic
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Ultramafic_rock" title="Ultramafic rock">
        ultramafic
       </a>
       (magnesium-rich) compositions. As a by-product of this activity, sulfur, sulfur dioxide gas and silicate
       <a href="/wiki/Pyroclastic_rock" title="Pyroclastic rock">
        pyroclastic
       </a>
       material (like ash) are blown up to 200 km (120 mi) into space, producing large, umbrella-shaped plumes, painting the surrounding terrain in red, black, and white, and providing material for Io's patchy atmosphere and Jupiter's extensive magnetosphere.
      </p>
      <p>
       Io's surface is dotted with volcanic depressions known as
       <i>
        paterae
       </i>
       which generally have flat floors bounded by steep walls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radebaugh2001_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Radebaugh2001-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These features resemble terrestrial
       <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">
        calderas
       </a>
       , but it is unknown if they are produced through collapse over an emptied lava chamber like their terrestrial cousins. One hypothesis suggests that these features are produced through the exhumation of volcanic
       <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">
        sills
       </a>
       , and the overlying material is either blasted out or integrated into the sill.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keszthelyi2004_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keszthelyi2004-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Examples of paterae in various stages of exhumation have been mapped using
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       images of the
       <a href="/wiki/Chaac-Camaxtli_region" title="Chaac-Camaxtli region">
        Chaac-Camaxtli region
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Williams-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike similar features on Earth and Mars, these depressions generally do not lie at the peak of
       <a href="/wiki/Shield_volcano" title="Shield volcano">
        shield volcanoes
       </a>
       and are normally larger, with an average diameter of 41 km (25 mi), the largest being
       <a href="/wiki/Loki_Patera" title="Loki Patera">
        Loki Patera
       </a>
       at 202 km (126 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radebaugh2001_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Radebaugh2001-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Loki is also consistently the strongest volcano on Io, contributing on average 25% of Io's global heat output.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Whatever the formation mechanism, the morphology and distribution of many paterae suggest that these features are structurally controlled, with at least half bounded by faults or mountains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radebaugh2001_86-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Radebaugh2001-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These features are often the site of volcanic eruptions, either from lava flows spreading across the floors of the paterae, as at an eruption at
       <a href="/wiki/Gish_Bar_Patera" title="Gish Bar Patera">
        Gish Bar Patera
       </a>
       in 2001, or in the form of a
       <a href="/wiki/Lava_lake" title="Lava lake">
        lava lake
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopes2004_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lopes2004-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry2003_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Perry2003-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Lava lakes on Io either have a continuously overturning lava crust, such as at Pele, or an episodically overturning crust, such as at Loki.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radebaugh2004_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Radebaugh2004-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howell2007_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Howell2007-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tvashtarvideo.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tvashtarvideo.gif/220px-Tvashtarvideo.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tvashtarvideo.gif/330px-Tvashtarvideo.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tvashtarvideo.gif/440px-Tvashtarvideo.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tvashtarvideo.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Five-image sequence of
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">
           New Horizons
          </a>
         </i>
         images showing Io's volcano Tvashtar spewing material 330 km above its surface.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Lava flows represent another major volcanic terrain on Io. Magma erupts onto the surface from vents on the floor of paterae or on the plains from fissures, producing inflated, compound lava flows similar to those seen at
       <a href="/wiki/K%C4%ABlauea" title="Kīlauea">
        Kilauea
       </a>
       in Hawaii. Images from the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       spacecraft revealed that many of Io's major lava flows, like those at
       <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(volcano)" title="Prometheus (volcano)">
        Prometheus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Amirani_(volcano)" title="Amirani (volcano)">
        Amirani
       </a>
       , are produced by the build-up of small breakouts of lava flows on top of older flows.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keszthelyi2001_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keszthelyi2001-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Larger outbreaks of lava have also been observed on Io. For example, the leading edge of the Prometheus flow moved 75 to 95 km (47 to 59 mi) between
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       in 1979 and the first
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       observations in 1996. A major eruption in 1997 produced more than 3,500 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (1,400 sq mi) of fresh lava and flooded the floor of the adjacent Pillan Patera.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcewen1998b_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mcewen1998b-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Analysis of the
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       images led scientists to believe that these flows were composed mostly of various compounds of molten sulfur. However, subsequent Earth-based
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        infrared
       </a>
       studies and measurements from the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       spacecraft indicate that these flows are composed of basaltic lava with mafic to ultramafic compositions. This hypothesis is based on temperature measurements of Io's "hotspots", or thermal-emission locations, which suggest temperatures of at least 1300 K and some as high as 1600 K.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keszthelyi2007_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keszthelyi2007-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Initial estimates suggesting eruption temperatures approaching 2000 K
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcewen1998b_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mcewen1998b-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have since proven to be overestimates because the wrong thermal models were used to model the temperatures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keszthelyi2007_94-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keszthelyi2007-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The discovery of plumes at the volcanoes
       <a href="/wiki/Pele_(volcano)" title="Pele (volcano)">
        Pele
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loki_(volcano)" title="Loki (volcano)">
        Loki
       </a>
       were the first sign that Io is geologically active.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morabito1979_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Morabito1979-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Generally, these plumes are formed when volatiles like sulfur and sulfur dioxide are ejected skyward from Io's volcanoes at speeds reaching 1 km/s (0.62 mi/s), creating umbrella-shaped clouds of gas and dust. Additional material that might be found in these volcanic plumes include sodium,
       <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">
        potassium
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">
        chlorine
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roesler1999_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Roesler1999-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geissler1999_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Geissler1999-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These plumes appear to be formed in one of two ways.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McEwen1983_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McEwen1983-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Io's largest plumes, such as those emitted by
       <a href="/wiki/Pele_(volcano)" title="Pele (volcano)">
        Pele
       </a>
       , are created when dissolved sulfur and sulfur dioxide gas are released from erupting magma at volcanic vents or lava lakes, often dragging silicate pyroclastic material with them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battaglia2014_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Battaglia2014-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These plumes form red (from the short-chain sulfur) and black (from the silicate pyroclastics) deposits on the surface. Plumes formed in this manner are among the largest observed at Io, forming red rings more than 1,000 km (620 mi) in diameter. Examples of this plume type include Pele, Tvashtar, and
       <a href="/wiki/Dazhbog_Patera" title="Dazhbog Patera">
        Dazhbog
       </a>
       . Another type of plume is produced when encroaching lava flows vaporize underlying sulfur dioxide frost, sending the sulfur skyward. This type of plume often forms bright circular deposits consisting of sulfur dioxide. These plumes are often less than 100 km (62 mi) tall, and are among the most long-lived plumes on Io. Examples include Prometheus, Amirani, and
       <a href="/wiki/Masubi_(volcano)" title="Masubi (volcano)">
        Masubi
       </a>
       . The erupted sulfurous compounds are concentrated in the upper crust from a decrease in sulfur solubility at greater depths in Io's lithosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battaglia2014_98-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Battaglia2014-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battaglia2015_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Battaglia2015-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mountains">
        Mountains
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Io_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mountains">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Mountains_of_Io" title="Mountains of Io">
        Mountains of Io
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_mountains_on_Io" title="List of mountains on Io">
        List of mountains on Io
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tohil_Mons.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="954" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Tohil_Mons.jpg/220px-Tohil_Mons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Tohil_Mons.jpg/330px-Tohil_Mons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Tohil_Mons.jpg/440px-Tohil_Mons.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tohil_Mons.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
           Galileo
          </a>
         </i>
         greyscale image of
         <a href="/wiki/Tohil_Mons" title="Tohil Mons">
          Tohil Mons
         </a>
         , a 5.4 km tall mountain
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Io has 100 to 150 mountains. These structures average 6 km (4 mi) in height and reach a maximum of 17.5 ± 1.5 km (10.9 ± 0.9 mi) at South
       <i>
        Boösaule Montes
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2001_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2001-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mountains often appear as large (the average mountain is 157 km or 98 mi long), isolated structures with no apparent global tectonic patterns outlined, in contrast to the case on Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2001_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2001-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To support the tremendous topography observed at these mountains requires compositions consisting mostly of silicate rock, as opposed to sulfur.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clow1980_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Clow1980-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite the extensive volcanism that gives Io its distinctive appearance, nearly all its mountains are tectonic structures, and are not produced by volcanoes. Instead, most Ionian mountains form as the result of compressive stresses on the base of the lithosphere, which uplift and often tilt chunks of Io's crust through
       <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">
        thrust faulting
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchenkBulmer1998_101-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SchenkBulmer1998-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The compressive stresses leading to mountain formation are the result of
       <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">
        subsidence
       </a>
       from the continuous burial of volcanic materials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchenkBulmer1998_101-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SchenkBulmer1998-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The global distribution of mountains appears to be opposite that of volcanic structures; mountains dominate areas with fewer volcanoes and vice versa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2001_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2001-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This suggests large-scale regions in Io's lithosphere where compression (supportive of mountain formation) and extension (supportive of patera formation) dominate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tackley2001_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tackley2001-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Locally, however, mountains and paterae often abut one another, suggesting that magma often exploits faults formed during mountain formation to reach the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radebaugh2001_86-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Radebaugh2001-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mountains on Io (generally, structures rising above the surrounding plains) have a variety of morphologies.
       <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">
        Plateaus
       </a>
       are most common.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2001_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2001-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These structures resemble large, flat-topped
       <a href="/wiki/Mesa" title="Mesa">
        mesas
       </a>
       with rugged surfaces. Other mountains appear to be tilted crustal blocks, with a shallow slope from the formerly flat surface and a steep slope consisting of formerly sub-surface materials uplifted by compressive stresses. Both types of mountains often have steep
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       along one or more margins. Only a handful of mountains on Io appear to have a volcanic origin. These mountains resemble small
       <a href="/wiki/Shield_volcano" title="Shield volcano">
        shield volcanoes
       </a>
       , with steep slopes (6–7°) near a small, central
       <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">
        caldera
       </a>
       and shallow slopes along their margins.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2004_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2004-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These volcanic mountains are often smaller than the average mountain on Io, averaging only 1 to 2 km (0.6 to 1.2 mi) in height and 40 to 60 km (25 to 37 mi) wide. Other shield volcanoes with much shallower slopes are inferred from the morphology of several of Io's volcanoes, where thin flows radiate out from a central patera, such as at
       <a href="/wiki/Ra_Patera" title="Ra Patera">
        Ra Patera
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2004_104-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk2004-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Nearly all mountains appear to be in some stage of degradation. Large
       <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">
        landslide
       </a>
       deposits are common at the base of Ionian mountains, suggesting that
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_wasting" title="Mass wasting">
        mass wasting
       </a>
       is the primary form of degradation. Scalloped margins are common among Io's mesas and plateaus, the result of sulfur dioxide
       <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_sapping" title="Groundwater sapping">
        sapping
       </a>
       from Io's crust, producing zones of weakness along mountain margins.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2001_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2001-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">
        Atmosphere
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Io_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Io_Aurorae_color.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="374" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg/220px-Io_Aurorae_color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg/330px-Io_Aurorae_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Io_Aurorae_color.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Auroral glows in Io's upper atmosphere. Different colors represent emission from different components of the atmosphere (green comes from emitting sodium, red from emitting oxygen, and blue from emitting volcanic gases like sulfur dioxide). Image taken while Io was in eclipse.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Io has an extremely thin
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       consisting mainly of sulfur dioxide (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ), with minor constituents including
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_monoxide" title="Sulfur monoxide">
        sulfur monoxide
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">
        sodium chloride
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        NaCl
       </span>
       ), and atomic
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
        sulfur
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        oxygen
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The atmosphere has significant variations in density and temperature with time of day, latitude, volcanic activity, and surface frost abundance. The maximum atmospheric pressure on Io ranges from 3.3 × 10
       <sup>
        −5
       </sup>
       to 3 × 10
       <sup>
        −4
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">
        pascals
       </a>
       (Pa) or 0.3 to 3
       <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">
        nbar
       </a>
       , spatially seen on Io's anti-Jupiter hemisphere and along the equator, and temporally in the early afternoon when the temperature of surface frost peaks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2010_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2010-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2005_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2005-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Localized peaks at volcanic plumes have also been seen, with pressures of 5 × 10
       <sup>
        −4
       </sup>
       to 40  × 10
       <sup>
        −4
       </sup>
       Pa (5 to 40 nbar).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearl1979_38-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pearl1979-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Io's atmospheric pressure is lowest on Io's night side, where the pressure dips to 0.1 × 10
       <sup>
        −7
       </sup>
       to 1 × 10
       <sup>
        −7
       </sup>
       Pa (0.0001 to 0.001 nbar).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2010_107-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2010-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Io's atmospheric temperature ranges from the temperature of the surface at low altitudes, where sulfur dioxide is in vapor pressure equilibrium with frost on the surface, to 1800 K at higher altitudes where the lower atmospheric density permits heating from plasma in the Io plasma torus and from Joule heating from the Io flux tube.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2010_107-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2010-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The low pressure limits the atmosphere's effect on the surface, except for temporarily redistributing sulfur dioxide from frost-rich to frost-poor areas, and to expand the size of plume deposit rings when plume material re-enters the thicker dayside atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2010_107-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2010-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The thin Ionian atmosphere also means any future landing probes sent to investigate Io will not need to be encased in an aeroshell-style heatshield, but instead require
       <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">
        retrothrusters
       </a>
       for a soft
       <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">
        landing
       </a>
       . The thin atmosphere also necessitates a rugged lander capable of enduring the strong Jovian
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">
        radiation
       </a>
       , which a thicker atmosphere would attenuate.
      </p>
      <p>
       Gas in Io's atmosphere is stripped by Jupiter's magnetosphere, escaping to either the neutral cloud that surrounds Io, or the Io plasma torus, a ring of
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ionized
       </a>
       particles that shares Io's orbit but co-rotates with the magnetosphere of Jupiter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krimigis2002_57-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krimigis2002-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Approximately one ton of material is removed from the atmosphere every second through this process so that it must be constantly replenished.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap11_54-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap11-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most dramatic source of
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       are volcanic plumes, which pump 10
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       kg of sulfur dioxide per second into Io's atmosphere on average, though most of this condenses back onto the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Much of the sulfur dioxide in Io's atmosphere sustained by sunlight-driven sublimation of
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       frozen on the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moullet2010_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moullet2010-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The day-side atmosphere is largely confined to within 40° of the equator, where the surface is warmest and most active volcanic plumes reside.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feaga2009_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Feaga2009-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A sublimation-driven atmosphere is also consistent with observations that Io's atmosphere is densest over the anti-Jupiter hemisphere, where
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       frost is most abundant, and is densest when Io is closer to the Sun.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moullet2010_110-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moullet2010-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlohaIo_112-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AlohaIo-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, some contributions from volcanic plumes are required as the highest observed densities have been seen near volcanic vents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_106-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because the density of sulfur dioxide in the atmosphere is tied directly to surface temperature, Io's atmosphere partially collapses at night, or when Io is in the shadow of Jupiter (with an ~80% drop in column density
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsang2016_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tsang2016-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). The collapse during eclipse is limited somewhat by the formation of a diffusion layer of sulfur monoxide in the lowest portion of the atmosphere, but the atmosphere pressure of Io's nightside atmosphere is two to four orders of magnitude less than at its peak just past noon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2010_107-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walker2010-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2009_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2009-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The minor constituents of Io's atmosphere, such as
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        NaCl
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        O
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        S
       </span>
       derive either from: direct volcanic outgassing;
       <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">
        photodissociation
       </a>
       , or chemical breakdown caused by solar ultraviolet radiation, from
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        SO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ; or the
       <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">
        sputtering
       </a>
       of surface deposits by charged particles from Jupiter's magnetosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moullet2010_110-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moullet2010-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       High-resolution images of Io acquired when Io is experiencing an eclipse reveal an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
        aurora
       </a>
       -like glow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geissler1999_96-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Geissler1999-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As on Earth, this is due to particle radiation hitting the atmosphere, though in this case the charged particles come from Jupiter's magnetic field rather than the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       . Aurorae usually occur near the magnetic poles of planets, but Io's are brightest near its equator. Io lacks an intrinsic magnetic field of its own; therefore, electrons traveling along Jupiter's magnetic field near Io directly impact Io's atmosphere. More electrons collide with its atmosphere, producing the brightest aurora, where the field lines are tangent to Io (i.e. near the equator), because the column of gas they pass through is longest there. Aurorae associated with these tangent points on Io are observed to rock with the changing orientation of Jupiter's tilted magnetic dipole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Retherford2000_115-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Retherford2000-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fainter aurora from oxygen atoms along the limb of Io (the red glows in the image at right), and sodium atoms on Io's night-side (the green glows in the same image) have also been observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geissler1999_96-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Geissler1999-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
             2
            </span>
           </span>
           atmosphere".
